NASA Science\",\"url\":\"https://science.nasa.gov/mission/pioneer-10/\"}]\n\nBased on the web search results, here's comprehensive information about Pioneer 11's NASA mission:\n\n## Launch Details\nPioneer 11 launched on April 6, 1973, from Cape Canaveral, United States, on an Atlas-Centaur rocket.\n\n## Spacecraft Specifications\nThe spacecraft had a mass of 259 kg with nominal power of 165 W. The spacecraft was 2.9 m long and contained a 2.74-m diameter high-gain antenna of aluminum honeycomb sandwich material.\n\n## Scientific Instruments\nPioneer 11 carried 11 scientific instruments to study Jupiter and its environment as well as interplanetary space. The spacecraft contained two nuclear electric-power generators, which generated 144 W at Jupiter, but decreased to 100 W at Saturn.\n\n## Jupiter Flyby\nPioneer 11's Jupiter flyby occurred on December 3, 1974. During its closest approach, Pioneer 11 passed to within 43,000 km of Jupiter's cloud tops.\n\n## Saturn Flyby\nPioneer 11's Saturn flyby took place on September 1, 1979. It passed by Saturn at a distance of 21,000 km from Saturn's cloud tops.\n\n## Mission Conclusion\nThe mission ended in 1995 and Pioneer 11 is on a trajectory to take it out of the solar system. Science operations and daily telemetry ceased on 30 September 1995 when the RTG power level was insufficient to operate any experiments.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b9d4fd9b-4306-47e8-be35-b7241058a071","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:49.843Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"1d9d3a51-5a5b-4907-9b4f-fe6bf2ef89e6","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/mission/skylab-4/\"},{\"title\":\"The Real Story of the Skylab 4 “Strike” in Space - NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/history/the-real-story-of-the-skylab-4-strike-in-space/\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here are the key NASA details about the Skylab space station launch in 1973:\n\n## Launch Details\n\nOn May 14, 1973, the final Saturn V rocket launched from Launch Pad 39A at NASA's Kennedy Space Center in Florida to lift the Skylab space station into orbit.\n\n## Mass and Dimensions\n\nOnce in orbit, the complex weighed 170,000 pounds, by far the heaviest spacecraft at that time.\n\n## Major Components and Structure\n\nThe Skylab complex consisted of four major components: the Orbital Workshop (OWS), the Airlock Module (AM), the Multiple Docking Adapter (MDA), and the Apollo Telescope Mount (ATM).\n\nThe OWS served as the main working, living, and sleeping compartment for the crews, and contained exercise equipment, a galley, and many of the scientific experiments. Two large solar arrays on the OWS provided 12.4 kW of power to the station.\n\n## Apollo Telescope Mount and Solar Observations\n\nThe ATM contained telescopes for solar observations and four solar arrays for additional power. The ATM solar telescopes took more than 170,000 images for astronomers, while Earth scientists received 46,000 photographs.\n\n## Scientific Experiments\n\nThree crews of three astronauts planned to spend 28, 56, and 56 days aboard the orbiting laboratory conducting 270 experiments. Weitz, and Science Pilot Joseph P. Kerwin composed the crew.\n\n## Mission Duration\nThe crew completed their record-setting 28-day mission on June 22, 1973, with a splashdown in the Pacific Ocean. More specifically, Conrad, Weitz and Kerwin were the new record holders for the longest human space flight, 28 days and 50 minutes.\n\n## Launch and Arrival\nThe Skylab 2 crew launched successfully from Kennedy Space Center's Launch Pad 39B on May 25, 1973, and six hours later approached Skylab.\n\n## Repair Operations\nThe mission had two critical repairs:\n\n1. **Parasol Sunshade**: They deployed a parasol-like sunshield from inside the station that lowered internal temperatures down to comfortable levels.\n\n2. **Solar Array Repair**: On June 7, 1973, on their 14th day in space, Conrad and Kerwin floated out of Skylab's Airlock Module. Conrad and Kerwin climbed back inside Skylab after 3 hours and 25 minutes, the longest spacewalk in Earth orbit to date.\n\n## Recovery\nThe Command Module splashed down about 800 miles southwest of San Diego and 6.5 miles from the prime recovery ship, the aircraft carrier USS Ticonderoga.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d6656eb4-3b6f-4aed-b9c6-11b4e4213ed4","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:52.555Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"17e8ecba-6c8d-4f42-bd08-2d6a76abe455","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Carr, Edward G. Gibson, and William R. Pogue splashed down in the Pacific Ocean on Feb. 8, 1974, after their 84-day mission aboard Skylab, which was America's first space station.\n\n## Crew\n\nGerald P. Carr was the mission commander and Marine Corps lieutenant colonel; Dr. Edward G. Gibson served as science pilot; and Lt. Col. William Pogue was the pilot. Notably, all three astronauts were making their first trip into space.\n\n## Mission Timeline and Duration\n\nSkylab 4 lifted off on November 16, 1973, at 9:01 a.m. from Kennedy Space Center. Docking eight hours later, they began a planned 56-day mission that program managers extended to a record-breaking 84 days.\n\n## Scientific Objectives and Experiments\n\nDuring their stay, they carried out a challenging research program, including biomedical investigations on the effects of long-duration space flight on the human body, Earth observations using the Earth Resources Experiment Package, and solar observations with instruments mounted in the Apollo Telescope Mount (ATM).\n\n## Comet Kohoutek Observations\n\nTo study newly discovered Comet Kohoutek, scientists added cometary observations to the crew's already busy schedule, including adding a far ultraviolet camera to Skylab's instrument suite. Carr, Gibson, and Pogue increased their focus on observing Comet Kohoutek as it neared perihelion on Dec. 28, and Carr and Gibson completed the mission's third spacewalk lasting 3 hours 29 minutes and dedicated to observing and photographing the comet.\n\n## Additional Accomplishments\n\nThe astronauts conducted four spacewalks, a then-record for a single Earth orbital mission, and completed significantly more science experiments than planned.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"158d6627-0dff-4def-8d92-6b94387eb355","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:55.343Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"12c28239-2a12-419e-b672-12dd18c56637","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The mission accomplished 150% of their mission goals while completing 858 revolutions of the Earth.\n\n## Crew\n\nThe Skylab 3 crew consisted of Commander Alan L. Bean, Pilot Jack R. Lousma, and Science Pilot Owen K. Garriott.\n\n## Mission Challenges and Achievements\n\nAfter overcoming leaky thrusters with their spacecraft, then bouts of space motion sickness, during a record-setting spacewalk they accomplished their first major task by deploying a second sunshade to help cool the space station, damaged at launch.\n\nTheir flight of 59 days and 11 hours more than doubled the previous 28-day record held by the Skylab 2 crew.\n\n## Spacewalks and Maintenance\n\nThe crew conducted three spacewalks during their mission:\n\n- During the 6-hour 31-minute extra vehicular activity (EVA), the longest Earth-orbital spacewalk up to that date, Garriott and Lousma deployed the twin-pole sunshade over the existing parasol and replaced film canisters in the Apollo Telescope Mount (ATM).\n\n- On August 24, Garriott and Lousma stepped outside for the second EVA of the mission, replacing ATM film canisters and installing a cable for a new set of gyroscopes they mounted inside the station.\n\n- During the third and final spacewalk September 22 lasting 2 hours and 41 minutes, Bean and Garriott retrieved all the exposed film cassettes from the ATM for return to Earth and installed some fresh ones.\n\n## Scientific Experiments\n\nThe biomedical experiments focused on better understanding their physiological responses to long-duration space flight, including changes to their cardiovascular, neurovestibular and musculoskeletal systems and their overall metabolism and nutrition. The crew members continued to monitor selected ground sites using multispectral instruments in the Earth Resources Experiment Package (EREP) and to make solar and astronomy observations with the ATM.\n\n## Return and Physical Condition\n\nThe longest human space flight up to that time ended with the splashdown of the Skylab 3 crew of Alan L. Bean, Owen K. Garriott, and Jack R. Lousma on September 25, 1973. Based on lessons learned from the first crew, such as the need for additional exercise and improved nutrition, the Skylab 3 crew returned in better physical condition despite their longer mission duration.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"43021be1-7481-4049-ada2-ce4d3d66ff90","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:28:59.641Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"9ef6390d-022a-4ef1-8ee0-3107b6affa19","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Additionally, Congress appropriated approximately $450 million for the Apollo Applications Program (AAP) in the 1968 Fiscal Year budget, which was the precursor to the Skylab program.\n\n### Orbital Parameters\n\nCircling 50 degrees north and south of the equator at an altitude of 435 km, Skylab had an orbital period of 93 minutes. More specifically, Skylab was inserted into a near-circular orbit at an altitude of 235 nautical miles with a nominal orbit inclination of 50 degrees to the Earth's equator.\n\n### Additional Technical Information\n\nOnce in orbit, the complex weighed 170,000 pounds, by far the heaviest spacecraft to date. The Skylab complex consisted of four major components: the Orbital Workshop (OWS), the Airlock Module (AM), the Multiple Docking Adapter (MDA), and the Apollo Telescope Mount (ATM).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"4dc71b2f-c84c-4353-b2b2-a304450a1a90","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:21.710Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"81f7f0ab-c199-4f02-a962-a43c4f7aecc7","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Skylab re-entered the Earth's atmosphere and disintegrated, dispersing debris across a sparsely populated section of western Australia and the southeastern Indian Ocean.\n\nThe station's decay was accelerated by environmental factors. Delays in the Shuttle program and higher than expected solar activity resulting in increased atmospheric drag on the station ultimately thwarted plans to boost the station to a higher orbit.\n\n## Skylab Dimensions and Volume\n\n**Overall Cluster Dimensions:**\nSkylab was 82.4 feet long and 36.3 feet high, with a diameter of 21.6 feet and pressurized volume of 12,417 cubic feet.\n\n**Orbital Workshop (OWS) Specifications:**\nThe Workshop measures 6.7 m (22 ft) in diameter, and 14.6 m (48 ft) in length. Its habitable volume is 275 m (approximately 9,700 cubic feet).\n\n**Station Mass:**\nAt 169,000 pounds, Skylab represented the heaviest spacecraft to reenter up to that time, though another source indicates the complex weighed 170,000 pounds, by far the heaviest spacecraft to date.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d87ebd52-6840-47a6-b7fd-ceeb1a98399d","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:23.372Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"87c7a475-5d48-4981-a696-222e78a9fc69","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The spacecraft had a mass of 473.9 kg, though another source mentions it weighed 500 kg.\n\n## Historical Significance\nMariner 10 was the first spacecraft sent to the planet Mercury; the first mission to explore two planets (Mercury and Venus) during a single mission; the first to return to its primary destination for another look; and the first to use a gravity assist to change its flight path.\n\n## Gravity Assist Maneuver\nUsing gravity assist, a technique theorized for decades but never used before, a spacecraft sent to one planet could use that planet's gravitational force to essentially slingshot to its next target. The method saved the cost of additional fuel and a larger rocket that would be necessary to launch the heavier spacecraft as well as time to get to the ultimate destination.\n\n## Venus and Mercury Flybys\nThe spacecraft passed within 5,768 kilometers of Venus on February 5, 1974, and sent back information about Venus' atmosphere and beaming back the first close-up images of the cloud-shrouded planet. The gravity assist was successful, placing the spacecraft on a trajectory toward Mercury which it encountered on March 29 at a distance of 703 km.\n\n## Scientific Instruments\nMariner 10 carried six scientific instruments – the Television Photography System consisting of two telescopes to image the planets; an infrared radiometer to calculate the temperature of Venus' atmosphere and Mercury's surface; an ultraviolet spectrometer primarily to detect any atmosphere around Mercury; plasma detectors to study the solar wind inside the orbit of Venus for the first time; a charged particle telescope to study cosmic radiation; and magnetometers to detect any magnetic field around Mercury.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"44fe69e0-1670-483f-9c40-0f8ac75d3032","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:38.832Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"73c565e6-7ed3-4431-a042-189364427872","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here's a comprehensive summary:\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nThe joint U.S.-USSR ASTP docking mission in Earth orbit took place in July 1975. Designed to test the compatibility of rendezvous and docking systems and the possibility of an international space rescue, the nine-day Apollo-Soyuz mission brought together two former spaceflight rivals: the United States and the Soviet Union.\n\n## Launch Dates and Crew\n\nThe Soviet Soyuz 19 spacecraft and rocket launched on July 15, 1975, at 9:20 a.m. EDT from the Baikonur Cosmodrome in Kazakhstan. On that same day, the Apollo 18 capsule atop the Saturn 1B rocket, designated AS-210, lifted off from Launch Pad 39B at Kennedy Space Center in Florida, at 3:50 p.m. EDT.\n\n**Apollo Crew:** Thomas P. Stafford, commander; Donald K. \"Deke\" Slayton, docking module pilot; Vance D. Brand, command module pilot.\n\n**Soyuz Crew:** Aleksey A. Leonov, command pilot; and Valery N. Kubasov, flight engineer.\n\n## Docking and Experiments\n\nThe Apollo spacecraft launched carrying a crew of three and docked two days later on July 17, with a Soyuz spacecraft and its crew of two. The Apollo and Soyuz spacecraft remained docked for about 47 hours. Several activities, four crew transfers and the completion of a number of joint scientific experiments and engineering investigations were achieved. During nearly two days of joint activities, the mission's two Soviet cosmonauts and three U.S. astronauts carried out five joint experiments and exchanged commemorative items.\n\n## Spacecraft Mass\n\nThe Apollo spacecraft mass was 14856 kg. The Soyuz spacecraft mass was 6800 kg.\n\n## Cost\n\nUnfortunately, the search results do not contain information about the project's total cost.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"a39bdd00-cd12-45c7-9cfe-6b7109cde0f5","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:39.148Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"37ab0a00-9754-4bf2-8061-b32652a8d5aa","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

NASA\",\"url\":\"https://www.nasa.gov/image-article/viking-1-spacecraft/\"}]\n\nBased on the search results, here is comprehensive information about the Viking 1 Mars mission:\n\n## Launch Information\nViking 1 launched on August 20, 1975, aboard a Titan IIIE-Centaur rocket from Cape Canaveral, Florida. The spacecraft had a mass of 883 kilograms.\n\n## Landing Site\nViking 1 landed on July 20, 1976, at Chryse Planitia (\"Golden Plain\"). The lander touched down at 22.27 degrees N latitude and 312.05 degrees E longitude (planetocentric) at 11:53:06 UT.\n\n## Spacecraft Configuration\nViking 1 consisted of both an orbiter and a lander designed to take high-resolution images, and study the Martian surface and atmosphere. The fully fueled orbiter-lander pair had a mass of 3530 kg. After separation and landing, the lander had a mass of about 600 kg and the orbiter 900 kg.\n\n## Biology Experiments\nThe two Viking landers conducted three biology experiments designed to look for possible signs of life. While it found no traces of life, Viking 1 did help better characterize Mars as a cold planet with volcanic soil, a thin, dry carbon dioxide atmosphere, and striking evidence for ancient river beds and vast flooding.\n\n## Mission Duration\nThe lander's mission ended on November 13, 1982, while the orbiter's mission ended on August 17, 1980.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"67b306e6-2caa-463e-b651-d37e28ceae6f","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:39.672Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"36d5980a-6428-48fe-859d-8757bbe04a52","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The landing site was at Utopia Planitia near the edge of the polar ice cap where water was located.\n\n## Mission Specifications\nThe lander had a mass of 883 kilograms and a nominal power of 620 Watts. After landing, the lander had a mass of about 600 kg.\n\n## Biology Experiments and Results\nThe biology experiments with scooped up soil collected on three occasions (beginning September 12, 1976) produced similar results to its twin: inconclusive on whether life exists or ever has existed on the surface of Mars. Scientists believed that Martian soil contained reactants created by the ultraviolet bombardment of the soil that could produce characteristics of living organisms in Earth soil.\n\n## Instruments\nThe lander carried two cameras as part of its imaging system, a seismometer, an x-ray fluorescence spectrometer, a weather instrument package to monitor temperature, pressure, and wind velocity, and a biological laboratory.\n\n## Mission Duration\nThe Viking 2 lander continued to transmit data until April 12, 1980, operating far longer than its original 90-day design lifetime.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"6609fda5-d343-4704-9d36-544043e76903","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:40.301Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"dbfda5d7-454f-4f2b-8025-442da460d3bd","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Let me compile the search results with proper citations.\n\n## Summary of Voyager 2 Spacecraft Information\n\n**Launch Date and Mission Background**\n\nVoyager 2 lifted off on Aug. 20, 1977, making it the first of the two spacecraft to be launched from the NASA Kennedy Space Center at Cape Canaveral, Florida.\n\n**Grand Tour Trajectory**\n\nTaking advantage of a rare planetary alignment to use the gravity of one planet to redirect the spacecraft to the next, the Voyagers initially targeted only Jupiter and Saturn, but Voyager 2 went on to explore Uranus and Neptune as well. The chosen flight path for Voyager 2 also preserved the option to continue on to Uranus and Neptune. Voyager 2 made successful flybys of Uranus (24 January 1986) and Neptune (25 August 1989).\n\n**Scientific Instruments**\n\nEach Voyager carried a suite of 11 instruments to study the planets during each encounter and to learn more about interplanetary space in the outer reaches of the solar system, including: An imaging science system consisting of narrow-angle and wide-angle cameras to photograph the planet and its satellites. A radio science system to determine the planet's physical properties. An infrared interferometer spectrometer to investigate local and global energy balance and atmospheric composition.\n\n**Radioisotope Thermoelectric Generator (RTG)**\n\nVoyager 2 is powered by Multi-Hundred Watt Radioisotope Thermoelectric Generators (MHW-RTGs) and warmed by nine radioisotope heater units (RHUs). The MHW-RTGs produced about 158 Watts each at launch in 1977. The RTGs used a radioactive source (Plutonium-238 in the form of plutonium oxide, or PuO2, in this case) which, as it decayed, gave off heat. A bi-metallic thermoelectric device was used to convert the heat to electric power for the spacecraft.\n\n**Golden Record**\n\nEach Voyager has mounted to one of the sides of the bus a 12-inch gold-plated copper disk. The disk has recorded on it sounds and images of Earth designed to portray the diversity of life and culture on the planet. The sound selections (including greetings in 55 languages, 35 sounds, natural and man-made, and portions of 27 musical pieces) are designed for playback at 1000 rpm.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"0373842e-050c-4b46-96f7-06cd96b858ba","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:29:47.297Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"7877a991-446b-4b74-84c4-0e092182d22a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"main","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Interestingly, Voyager 2 was launched first, on August 20, 1977, but because of a faster route, Voyager 1 overtook Voyager 2 on December 15, 1977.\n\n## Mission to Jupiter and Saturn\n\nVoyager 1 was launched in September 1977 and flew by Jupiter and Saturn. Voyager 1 arrived at Jupiter on March 5, 1979, and Saturn on November 12, 1980.\n\n## Key Discoveries\n\n**At Jupiter:**\nVoyager 1 discovered a thin ring around Jupiter and two new Jovian moons: Thebe and Metis.\n\n**At Saturn:**\nAt Saturn, Voyager 1 found five new moons and a new ring called the G-ring.\n\n## Fastest and Farthest Human-Made Object\n\nNo spacecraft has gone farther than NASA's Voyager 1. Voyager 1 crossed into interstellar space in August 2012 and is now in interstellar space, the region outside the heliopause, or the bubble of energetic particles and magnetic fields from the Sun.\n\n## Instruments\n\nVoyager 1 continues to communicate with NASA's Deep Space Network and send data back from four still-functioning instruments — the cosmic ray telescope, the low-energy charged particles experiment, the magnetometer, and the plasma waves experiment.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"37c6560d-5957-492d-b21b-68ea78244ac3","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:01.228Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"a8aa2b91-7d46-4efa-94ea-7429df4dec1a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Another source indicates the Orbiter carried 17 scientific instruments.\n\n## Orbit Characteristics\nThe initial orbital period was 23 hours, 11 minutes, which was altered within two orbits to the desired 24 hours—a maneuver that would allow the orbit's high and low points (about 99 miles or 160 kilometers) to occur at the same time each Earth day.\n\n## Mission Objectives\nIts primary objective was to investigate the solar wind in the Venusian environment, map Venus' surface through a radar imaging system, and study the characteristics of the upper atmosphere and ionosphere.\n\n## Venus Atmospheric Measurements\nPioneer Venus Orbiter measured the detailed structure of the upper atmosphere and ionosphere of Venus, investigated the interaction of the solar wind with the ionosphere and the magnetic field in the vicinity of Venus, determined the characteristics of the atmosphere and surface of Venus on a planetary scale.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"f0ebf004-8103-4bbc-81e2-3c403b04d81b","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:02.848Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"132b1838-0b81-42e4-b7e6-c816ef823602","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

the NSSDCA\",\"url\":\"https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/spacecraft/display.action?id=1978-078G\"}]\n\nBased on the web search results, here are the details of the Pioneer Venus Multiprobe 1978 mission:\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nThe Pioneer Venus Multiprobe launched on August 8, 1978, and consisted of a bus that carried one large and three small atmospheric probes.\n\n## Probe Release and Atmospheric Entry\n\nThe large probe was released on November 16, 1978 and the three small probes on November 20. All four probes entered the Venus atmosphere on December 9, followed by the bus.\n\n## Large Probe Details\n\nThe Large Probe was 5 feet (1.5 meters) in diameter and weighed 698 pounds (316.5 kilograms). The Large Probe descended through the atmosphere, entering at a velocity of 7 miles per second (11.6 kilometers per second), slowing down until it impacted on the surface of Venus at 19:40 UT, landing at 4.4 degrees north latitude and 304 degrees east longitude at a velocity of 20 miles per hour (32 kilometers per hour).\n\nThe Large Probe carried a neutral mass spectrometer, a solar flux radiometer, an atmospheric structure experiment, a nephelometer, a cloud particle size spectrometer, a gas chromatograph, an infrared radiometer, and radio science experiments.\n\n## Small Probes Details\n\nThe three small probes were identical, 0.8 m in diameter, and consisted of spherical pressure vessels surrounded by an aeroshell, but unlike the large probe, they had no parachutes and the aeroshells did not separate from the probe. Each small probe carried a nephelometer and temperature, pressure, and acceleration sensors, as well as a net flux radiometer experiment to map the distribution of sources and sinks of radiative energy in the atmosphere.\n\n## Survival Time and Results\n\nThe so-called Day Probe transmitted data from the surface for 67 minutes, 37 seconds, before succumbing to the high temperatures, pressures and power depletion. Amazingly, two of the three Small Probes survived the hard impact.\n\n## Atmospheric Measurements\n\nData from the probes indicated that between about 6 and 31 miles (10 and 50 kilometers) there is almost no convection in the atmosphere of Venus. Below a haze layer at about 19 miles (30 kilometers), the atmosphere is relatively clear. In addition, below an altitude of 31 miles (50 kilometers), the temperatures reported from the four probes indicated very few differences even though their entry sites were separated by thousands of miles.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d244e0cc-8893-4126-a02e-9a66f23005b1","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:05.502Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"eeb0e8e4-77d9-4107-94b3-8a83e4fdc71c","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

HEASARC - NASA\",\"url\":\"https://heasarc.gsfc.nasa.gov/docs/journal/heao1-a2_5.html\"},{\"title\":\"HEAO 1 - Spacecraft - the NSSDCA\",\"url\":\"https://nssdc.gsfc.nasa.gov/nmc/spacecraft/display.action?id=1977-075A\"},{\"title\":\"HEASARC: Observatories\",\"url\":\"https://heasarc.gsfc.nasa.gov/docs/observatories.html\"},{\"title\":\"The High Energy Astrophysics Observatory-3 (HEAO-3)\",\"url\":\"https://heasarc.gsfc.nasa.gov/docs/heao3/heao3.html\"}]\n\nHere are the search results for HEAO-1 (High Energy Astronomy Observatory 1):\n\n## Launch and Mission Overview\n\nHEAO-1 was launched on August 12, 1977, aboard an Atlas/Centaur launch vehicle. It was the first in a series of three spacecraft in the HEAO Program and was designed to survey the sky for additional X-ray and gamma-ray sources and to pinpoint their positions. The mission lasted 17 months.\n\n## Instruments\n\nThe observatory carried four experiments: Large Area X-ray Survey, Cosmic X-ray Background Experiment, Scanning Modulation Collimator, and Hard X-ray and Low Energy Gamma-Ray Experiment. These instruments operated across an energy range from 0.2 keV to 10 MeV.\n\n## Spacecraft Specifications\n\nThe six-sided HEAO 1 was 5.68 m high and 2.67 m in diameter, and weighed 2552 kg, which included 1220-kg experiments. It was launched into a nearly circular orbit with apogee 445 km, inclination 22.75°, and orbital period approximately 93 minutes.\n\n## Scientific Contributions\n\nHEAO-1 surveyed the X-ray sky almost three times over the 0.2 keV to 10 MeV range and provided nearly continuous monitoring of X-ray sources near the ecliptic poles. Identified sources fell into several categories, including: narrow emission line galaxies, broad emission line galaxies, BL Lacertae objects, and clusters of galaxies.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b557a801-8c21-4fd2-bfcd-12e6dd8768db","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:07.064Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"e1bb8200-b117-4f0f-832a-89b7200f9ec9","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

After launch, ISEE-3 was successfully placed in a halo orbit around the L1 Sun-Earth Lagrange point on the sunward side of Earth, about 932,000 miles (1.5 million kilometers) from Earth where the gravitational forces of Earth and the Sun are exactly counterbalanced.\n\n## Spacecraft Mass\n\nThe spacecraft had a mass of 390 kg with nominal power of 173 W.\n\n## Giacobini-Zinner Comet Flyby\n\nAfter a proposal by NASA scientist Robert Farquhar, NASA agreed in August 1982 to redirect the spacecraft for a rendezvous with Comet 21P/Giacobini-Zinner. After a series of five complex flybys of the Moon (the last on Dec. 22, 1983, at a range of about 75 miles or 120 kilometers), ISEE-3 was sent on a trajectory to encounter the comet. At this point, the spacecraft was renamed the International Cometary Explorer (ICE). On Sept. 11, 1985, at 11:02 UT, ICE passed within 4,885 miles (7,862 kilometers) of the comet's nucleus, becoming the first spacecraft to fly past a comet.\n\n## Instruments\n\nThe X-ray spectrometer was designed to study both solar flares and cosmic gamma-ray bursts. The experiment consisted of 2 cylindrical X-ray detectors: a Xenon filled proportional counter covering 5–14 keV, and a NaI(Tl) scintillator covering 12–1250 keV. The proportional counter was 1.27 cm in diameter and was filled with a mixture of 97% Xenon and 3% carbon dioxide. The central part of the counter body was made of 0.51 mm thick beryllium and served as the X-ray entrance window. The Ion Composition Instrument (ICI) made observations from September 1978 to the end of 1982.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"8aa5303a-fe09-4758-b1c4-2a00c520b4c2","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:08.714Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"ff685271-6186-4e5c-88ea-822aad7b2911","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

HEAO-2, the second of three HEAO satellites, was renamed Einstein after launch and was the first fully imaging X-ray telescope put into space.\n\n## Technical Specifications\n\nThe Einstein (HEAO 2) X-ray Observatory includes a fully imaging focusing X-ray telescope with an angular resolution of a few arc sec, a field of view of up to one deg, and a sensitivity several hundred times greater than previously available in any X-ray astronomy experiment. The total spacecraft mass was 3130 kg.\n\n## Imaging X-ray Instruments\n\nA high-resolution imager, an imaging proportional counter, a focal plane crystal spectrometer, and a monitor proportional counter are among the principal instruments on board the Einstein X-ray Observatory. A large grazing-incidence X-ray telescope provided images of sources that were then analyzed by four interchangeable instruments mounted on a carousel arrangement, which were a solid-state spectrometer (SSS), a focal plan crystal spectrometer (FPCS), an imaging proportional counter (IPC), and a high-resolution imaging detector (HRI).\n\n## Key Discoveries and Scientific Impact\n\nDuring its 2.5-yr mission, the Einstein X-Ray Telescope was pointed toward some 5,000 celestial targets, most of which were detected, and discovered several thousand additional 'serendipitous' sources in the observed fields. It was the first X-ray observatory to include a Guest Observer Program, which enabled a broad community of scientists to make use of its facilities.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"cb65860c-0388-49a0-b4ac-12b5b64eafab","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:22.300Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"1c842fe5-33b1-436a-82e9-67284fd5c0c1","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

The satellite was launched into an orbit of 500 km altitude, 43.6 degrees inclination.\n\n## Mission Objectives\nHEAO-3 continued the program's mission of probing the electromagnetic spectrum, performing a sky survey of gamma rays and cosmic rays in a manner similar to HEAO-1.\n\n## Instruments\nThe mission included two cosmic ray experiments, the Heavy Nuclei Experiment, the Cosmic Ray Isotope Experiment, and the Gamma-Ray Spectroscopy Experiment. \n\nThe Gamma Ray Spectroscopy Experiment on HEAO-3 consisted of four p-type, high purity germanium detectors, each with a volume of ~100 cm3, with an energy range of 50 keV-10 MeV.\n\n## Discoveries\nOn 1979 November 16, two gamma-ray bursts were observed by the High-Resolution Gamma-Ray Spectrometer on HEAO-3. Here's a summary of the detailed findings:\n\n## Overview of the Viking Biology Experiments\n\nThe Viking biology experiments searched for the presence of Martian organisms by looking for metabolic products using three distinct instruments: pyrolytic release (PR), labeled release (LR), and gas exchange (GEX).\n\n## Detailed Experiment Descriptions\n\n**Pyrolytic Release (PR) Experiment:**\nThe PR, or carbon assimilation, instrument sought to detect the photosynthetic or chemical fixation of CO2 or CO containing C-14, with samples incubated for several days in the presence of the radioactive gas mixture, some samples with simulated sunlight and some without. Each sample was heated to 120°C to remove unreacted CO2 and CO, the soil was pyrolized at 650°C and any organic products were collected in an organic vapor trap, which was then heated to combust the organic material to CO2 and any evolved radioactive gas was measured.\n\n**Labeled Release (LR) Experiment:**\nThe LR experiment sought to detect metabolic processes through radiorespirometry, with liquid nutrients labeled with radioactive carbon added to the samples and the atmosphere above continuously monitored to detect any radioactive gases released from these nonvolatile nutrients.\n\n**Gas Exchange (GEX) Experiment:**\nThe GEX measured the production and/or uptake of CO2, N2, CH4, H2, and O2 during incubation of a soil sample sealed and purged by He; then a mixture of He, Kr, and CO2 was introduced as an initial incubation atmosphere and after nutrient solution addition, samples of the atmosphere were removed at certain intervals and analyzed by a gas chromatograph with a thermal conductivity detector.\n\n## Key Results\n\nThe experiments discovered unexpected and enigmatic chemical activity in the Martian soil, but provided no clear evidence for the presence of living microorganisms in soil near the landing sites. The GCMS did not detect any organic compounds.\n\n## Later Interpretations\n\nAlthough initially formulated as biology experiments, most of the results produced by the Viking LR, GEX, and PR experiments have been reproduced by chemical means. The discovery of perchlorate on Mars by the Phoenix mission has provided a basis for explaining the results of the Viking Landers, with thermal decomposition of perchlorate explaining the lack of organics detected and accumulation of hypochlorite in the soil explaining the reactivity seen when nutrient solutions were added to the soil.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"feff4079-4d61-4031-8295-75def26a6fd6","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:27.594Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"ce1ea40b-f8ca-4713-b308-563f4bbc1632","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Measures energetic particles and cosmic rays from 3-110 MeV, detecting both solar and galactic sources.\n\n2. **Low-Energy Charged Particles (LECP)** - Detects intermediate-energy particles using solid-state detector systems (LEPT and LEMPA subsystems).\n\n3. **Magnetometer (MAG)** - \"Measure and analytically represent the planetary magnetic fields of Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune.\"\n\n4. **Plasma Wave Subsystem (PWS)** - Covers frequency range of 10 Hz to 56 kHz using shared antenna system.\n\n## Inactive Instruments\n\n5. **Plasma Science (PLS)** - Measured low-energy ions and electrons comprising bulk plasma properties.\n\n6. **Imaging Science Subsystem (ISS)** - Dual television cameras with wide-angle (200mm) and narrow-angle (1500mm) lenses.\n\n7. **Infrared Interferometer Spectrometer and Radiometer (IRIS)** - Functioned as thermometer, spectral analyzer, and radiometer.\n\n8. **Photopolarimeter Subsystem (PPS)** - Used 0.2m telescope measuring intensity and polarization at eight wavelengths (235-750nm).\n\n9. **Planetary Radio Astronomy (PRA)** - Receiver covering 20.4 kHz to 1300 kHz and 2.3 MHz to 40.5 MHz frequency bands.\n\n10. **Ultraviolet Spectrometer (UVS)** - \"Sensitive to ultraviolet light\" detecting specific atomic emissions (40-180nm wavelength)."}]},"uuid":"63d0b6d7-d85f-4835-aca8-ebd88040452e","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:30:40.717Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"9905a7d7-b162-4964-9824-689d0c232451","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Voyager Mission Technical Specifications\n\n## Launch Information\n- **Voyager 2**: August 20, 1977\n- **Voyager 1**: September 5, 1977\n- Both launched from Cape Canaveral, Florida aboard Titan-Centaur rockets\n\n## Planetary Encounters\n\n**Jupiter:**\n- Voyager 1: March 5, 1979 (277,400 km closest approach)\n- Voyager 2: July 9, 1979 (650,180 km closest approach)\n\n**Saturn:**\n- The amount of money the Soviet Union spent on the ASTP is unknown but reportedly constituted a \"significant portion.\"\n\n## Mission Overview\n\nOn July 15, 1975, an Apollo spacecraft launched carrying a crew of three and docked two days later on July 17, with a Soyuz spacecraft and its crew of two. The mission was designed to test the compatibility of rendezvous and docking systems and the possibility of an international space rescue.\n\n## Docking Module\n\nThe Marshall Space Flight Center in Huntsville, AL handled much of the technical work of designing the docking module that linked the Apollo and Soyuz crafts.\n\n## On-Orbit Operations and Experiments\n\nThe ships were docked for 1 day, 23 hours, seven minutes, and three seconds. After 44 hours together, the two ships separated, and maneuvered to use the Apollo to create an artificial solar eclipse to allow the crew of the Soyuz to take photographs of the solar corona. It used Venus to bend its flight path and bring its perihelion down to the level of Mercury's orbit. Mariner 10 was the first mission to use gravity assist and demonstrated this technique for reaching planetary targets with a minimum of launch energy.\n\n## Attitude Control Innovation\n\nSome new procedures were used to orient the spacecraft, including Sun-line maneuvers and the use of solar wind on the solar panels to orient the spacecraft. Additionally, the solar panels, power subsystem, attitude control subsystem, and the computer kept the spacecraft operating properly during the flight.\n\n## Spacecraft Design\n\nThe planned gravity assist at Venus made it feasible to use an Atlas-Centaur rocket instead of a more powerful but more expensive Titan IIIC. The budget constraint was significant enough that the budget did not permit both to be launched at the same time when considering a backup spacecraft.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"c9f4de71-a677-4f0d-9e0c-b82e9288060f","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:31:34.175Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"434b5346-6fb4-49a5-b436-fe09a3d36e51","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Voyager Golden Record: Comprehensive Overview\n\n## Physical Description\nThe Golden Record is \"a 12-inch gold-plated copper disk containing sounds and images selected to portray the diversity of life and culture on Earth.\" It functions as a phonograph record designed for playback by extraterrestrial recipients.\n\n## Purpose & Context\nLaunched aboard both Voyager 1 and 2 in 1977, the record serves as \"a kind of time capsule, intended to communicate a story of our world to extraterrestrials.\" This concept built upon earlier Pioneer missions that carried metal plaques identifying their origin for potential alien finders.\n\n## Contents Overview\nThe record includes:\n- **Images**: Multiple visual representations of Earth and human life\n- **Spoken greetings**: \"greetings in 55 languages\"\n- **Natural sounds**: Including thunder and bird vocalizations\n- **Musical selections**: \"musical selections from different cultures and eras\"\n\n## The Record's Cover\nThe protective Each of the two identical cameras on each orbiter had mechanical shutters; a 475-mm focal length telescope; a 37-mm diameter vidicon, the central section of which was scanned in a raster format of 1056 lines by 1182 samples.\n\n### Infrared Thermal Mapper (IRTM)\n\nThe IRTM was a multichannel radiometer mounted on the orbiter's scan platform. Four small telescopes, each with seven infrared detectors, were aimed parallel to the visual imaging optical axis, and made observations every 1.12 seconds. The purpose of the IRTM experiment was to measure the temperatures of the atmosphere and areas on the surface of Mars.\n\n### Mars Atmospheric Water Detection (MAWD)\n\nThe MAWD used an infrared grating spectrometer mounted on the orbiter scan platform that was boresighted with the television cameras and the Infrared Thermal Mapper. The instrument measured solar infrared radiation reflected from the surface through the atmosphere to the spacecraft.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"f19e8244-ca7f-496f-8df8-76ad0b67e634","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:10.011Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"a57b18e5-1834-4fa5-bf79-b78761805703","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here's a summary based on the search results:\n\n## Viking Lander Instruments\n\nEach Viking lander carried two cameras as part of its imaging system, a seismometer, an x-ray fluorescence spectrometer, a weather instrument package to monitor temperature, pressure, and wind velocity, and a biological laboratory.\n\n### Key Scientific Instruments:\n\n**Gas Chromatograph Mass Spectrometer (GCMS):**\nA gas chromatograph mass spectrometer (GCMS) measured the composition of the atmosphere and searched for organic compounds in the soils.\n\n**X-ray Fluorescence Spectrometer (XRFS):**\nAn X-ray fluorescence spectrometer (XRFS) measured the inorganic elemental composition of soils at the landing sites.\n\n**Seismometer:**\nA three-axis seismometer will measure any seismic activity that takes place during the mission, which should establish whether or not Mars is a very active planet.\n\n**Meteorology Instruments:**\nThe meteorology instrument, located on a folding boom attached to the Lander, will periodically measure temperature, pressure, wind speed and direction during the mission.\n\n**Cameras:**\nAn imaging system that consisted of two identical cameras.\n\n**Biology Investigation:**\nThe two Viking landers conducted three biology experiments designed to look for possible signs of life. The gas exchange experiment looked for gases given off by a sample of Martian soil incubated in a mix of organic and inorganic nutrients, the labeled release experiment involved placing a nutrient solution tagged with a 14C label on a soil sample and monitoring for the release of any 14CO2, and the pyrolytic release experiment placed 14C labeled gasses in a chamber with Martian soil to look for signs of incorporation that would indicate photosynthetic activity.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"81c3fb83-6471-48bb-869d-e842b20d2edd","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:11.635Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"cfddcc77-70fc-42cb-91da-e34ae8bcd67e","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

It was the first time active volcanoes had been seen on another body in the solar system. Together, the Voyagers observed the eruption of nine volcanoes on Io, and there is evidence that other eruptions occurred between the Voyager encounters. Plumes from the volcanoes extend to more than 300 kilometers (190 miles) above the surface.\n\nIo's volcanoes are apparently due to heating of the satellite by tidal pumping. Io is perturbed in its orbit by Europa and Ganymede, two other large satellites nearby, then pulled back again into its regular orbit by Jupiter. This tug-of-war results in tidal bulging as great as 100 meters (330 feet) on Io's surface.\n\n## Europa's Ocean\n\nThe moon Europa has a geologically young surface made of ice possibly floating on an ocean of liquid water. Europa displayed a large number of intersecting linear features in the low-resolution photos from Voyager 1. At first, scientists believed the features might be deep cracks, caused by crustal rifting or tectonic processes. The closer high-resolution photos from Voyager 2, however, left scientists puzzled: The features were so lacking in topographic relief that as one scientist described them, they \"might have been painted on with a felt marker.\"\n\n## Jupiter's Rings and Great Red Spot\n\nIn early 1979, Voyager 1 discovered a faint ring system around Jupiter. Jupiter's Great Red Spot is a large swirling storm system.\n\nThe spacecraft began its encounter phase with the Jovian system on Jan. 6, 1979. About five hours later, Voyager 1 made its closest approach to Jupiter, flying within 174,000 miles of the planet's cloud tops.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"8610df70-fca8-4db0-864c-6d5108a9c253","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:28.888Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"0ec69f47-6362-40e8-bea6-ff4e38dfb338","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here are the key findings:\n\n## Viking X-ray Fluorescence Results\n\nTen samples of the Martian regolith were analyzed by the Viking lander X-ray fluorescence spectrometers. Because of high-stability electronics, inclusion of calibration targets, and special data encoding within the instruments, the quality of the analyses performed on Mars was closely equivalent to that attainable with the same instruments operated in the laboratory.\n\n## Key Compositional Findings\n\nChemical results from four samples of Martian fines delivered to Viking landers 1 and 2 were remarkably similar in that they all had high iron; moderate magnesium, calcium, and sulfur; low aluminum; and apparently very low alkalies and trace elements.\n\nThe Martian fines were lower in aluminum, iron, sulfur, and chlorine than typical terrestrial continental soils or lunar mare fines.\n\n## Regional Homogeneity\n\nBulk fines at both Viking landing sites were quite similar in composition, implying that a chemically and mineralogically homogeneous regolith covered much of the surface of the planet.\n\n## Mineralogical Interpretation\n\nA mineralogic model derived from computer mixing studies and laboratory analog preparations suggests that Mars fines could be an intimate mixture of about 80% iron-rich clay, about 10% magnesium sulfate (kieserite), about 5% carbonate (calcite), and about 5% iron oxides (hematite, magnetite, maghemite, goethite).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"b2adfbdf-5635-458f-b7ec-e8e47e5c1588","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:29.041Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"fb344b02-8826-42a6-ac4d-18136f8d4fa9","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}

Here's a summary of the key findings:\n\n## HEAO-1 Mission Summary\n\n**Mission End Date:** The HEAO-1 mission operated from 12 August 1977 to 9 January 1979. On 9 January 1979, the gas used to control the spacecraft attitude ran out, and the systems were shutdown. HEAO-1 drifted in a decaying orbit until March 1979, when it burned up on re-entry into the atmosphere.\n\n**Orbit Parameters:** The HEAO-1 satellite was launched into a nearly circular orbit with apogee 445 km, inclination 22.75°, and orbital period approximately 93 minutes. HEAO-1 was primarily a scanning mission; it rotated once per 30 minutes about the Earth-Sun line.\n\n**Key Discoveries and Instruments:** HEAO-1 surveyed the X-ray sky almost three times over the 0.2 keV to 10 MeV range and provided nearly continuous monitoring of X-ray sources near the ecliptic poles.\n\n**X-ray Catalog - Sources Detected:** The HEAO 1 A-1 X-Ray Source Catalog contains information about X-ray sources detected with the NRL Large Area Sky Survey Experiment (also referred to as the A-1 Experiment) flown aboard the HEAO-1 satellite. The catalog represents results from the first six months of data from HEAO-1, and positions and intensities for 842 sources are listed, with a limiting flux of 250 nanoJanskies (nJy) at 5 keV.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"affb0528-a489-40aa-9f6b-7b488a7fd2b0","timestamp":"2026-03-29T19:32:54.549Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"82057e6d-2693-4b46-ba26-4bfa771ef8d0","userType":"external","entrypoint":"cli","cwd":"/path/to/projectGSD/dev-tools/gsd-skill-creator","sessionId":"eb657217-f115-4cdc-98b5-170cfdd68c16","version":"2.1.87","gitBranch":"dev","slug":"cheerful-churning-whisper"}
