Let me compile the complete structured enrichment data.\n\n---\n\n# NASA Post-Apollo Epoch 3 (1973-1980) — Mission Research Catalog v1.84-1.100\n\n## 1.84 — Pioneer 11\n\n**Mission Type:** Jupiter/Saturn flyby (outer planet reconnaissance)\n**NSSDC ID:** 1973-019A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | April 6, 1973, 02:11 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas-Centaur (AC-30) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral, LC-36B |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 258.5 kg (570 lbs) |\n| **Power System** | Two SNAP-19 RTGs; 155 W at launch, 144 W at Jupiter, 100 W at Saturn |\n| **End Date** | September 30, 1995 (last contact); November 24, 1995 (final engineering data) |\n| **Total Duration** | ~22 years active |\n| **Cost** | ~$175M (Pioneer 10+11 combined: $350M total; ~$2.4B in 2023 dollars) |\n\n**Instruments (12):**\n1. Imaging Photopolarimeter\n2. Helium Vector Magnetometer\n3. Infrared Radiometer\n4. Quadrispherical Plasma Analyzer\n5. Ultraviolet Photometer\n6. Charged Particle Instrument\n7. Cosmic Ray Telescope\n8. Geiger Tube Telescope\n9. Sisyphus Asteroid/Meteoroid Detector\n10. Meteoroid Detectors\n11. Trapped Radiation Detector\n12. Triaxial Fluxgate Magnetometer\n\n**Trajectory/Encounters:**\n- Traversed asteroid belt safely, mid-March 1974\n- **Jupiter flyby:** December 3, 1974 — closest approach 42,500 km (26,400 mi) from cloud tops at 171,000 km/h; polar trajectory provided first views of Jupiter's poles\n- **Saturn flyby:** September 1, 1979 — closest approach 20,900 km (13,000 mi) from cloud tops at 114,000 km/h; 440 images captured\n- Crossed Neptune's orbit: February 23, 1990\n- At shutdown: 44.1 AU from Earth\n- Heading toward constellation Sagittarius (galactic center); will pass near Lambda Aquilae in ~4 million years\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- First spacecraft to fly by Saturn\n- Discovered Saturn's F ring\n- Discovered a new Saturn moon (~124 miles diameter)\n- Confirmed Saturn's magnetic field via bow shock detection\n- Measured Titan temperature: -193C\n- Saturn atmospheric temperature: -180C\n- First polar images of Jupiter\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First spacecraft to encounter Saturn\n- First to provide close-up images of Saturn's rings\n- Pioneer plaque (designed by Carl Sagan) carried as message to extraterrestrial civilizations\n\n---\n\n## 1.85 — Skylab (Station Launch / OWS)\n\n**Mission Type:** Space station (first US)\n**NSSDC ID:** 1973-027A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | May 14, 1973, 17:30 UTC (13:30 EDT) |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Saturn V (SA-513) — final Saturn V flight |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39A |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 77,088 kg (170,000 lbs) — heaviest spacecraft at that time |\n| **Dimensions** | 36.1 m long overall, 6.7 m diameter (OWS); pressurized volume 12,417 cu ft (351.6 m3) |\n| **Orbit** | 435 km altitude, 50 deg inclination, 93-min period |\n| **Power** | OWS solar arrays: 12.4 kW; ATM solar arrays: additional power (4 panels) |\n| **Reentry** | July 11, 1979, orbit 34,981 — debris scattered over Indian Ocean and Western Australia |\n| **Total Duration** | 6 years 2 months in orbit; 9 months crewed |\n| **Cost** | ~$2.2 billion (total program) |\n\n**Major Components:**\n1. **Orbital Workshop (OWS)** — living/working quarters, 6.7 m diameter, 14.6 m length, 275 m3 habitable volume\n2. **Airlock Module (AM)** — EVA access\n3. **Multiple Docking Adapter (MDA)** — 2 docking ports, Earth Resources Experiment Package (EREP)\n4. **Apollo Telescope Mount (ATM)** — solar observatory with 4 solar panels\n\n**ATM Instruments (9):**\n\n| Designation | Type | Institution |\n|---|---|---|\n| S-052 | White Light Coronagraph | High Altitude Observatory |\n| S-054 | X-ray Telescope | AS&E |\n| S-055 | UV Spectroheliometer | Harvard College Observatory |\n| S-056 | X-ray Telescope (6-49 A) | MSFC |\n| S-020 | X-ray/EUV Camera | Naval Research Lab |\n| S-082A | EUV Spectroheliograph | Naval Research Lab |\n| S-082B | UV Spectroheliograph | Naval Research Lab |\n| H-alpha 1 | Hydrogen-alpha Telescope | Harvard College Observatory |\n| H-alpha 2 | Hydrogen-alpha Telescope | MSFC |\n\n**Scientific Output:**\n- 270 experiments across three crews\n- ATM: 170,000+ solar images\n- EREP: 46,000 Earth photographs\n- Coverage: 2-7000 Angstroms (soft X-ray through visible light)\n\n**Launch Damage:**\n- 63 seconds after launch, micrometeoroid shield tore away, ripping off one OWS solar array and jamming the other\n- Internal temperatures rose to 54C (130F)\n- Required emergency repair by Skylab 2 crew\n\n**Technology Innovations:**\n- First US space station\n- Converted S-IVB upper stage into habitable space\n- Proved long-duration spaceflight feasible (up to 84 days)\n- ATM was first manned solar observatory in space\n\n---\n\n## 1.86 — Skylab 2 (SL-2) — First Crewed Mission / Repair\n\n**Mission Type:** Crewed space station mission (repair + science)\n**NSSDC ID:** 1973-032A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | May 25, 1973, 13:00 UTC (9:00 AM EDT) |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Saturn IB (SA-206) |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39B |\n| **Splashdown** | June 22, 1973, Pacific Ocean (6.5 mi from USS Ticonderoga) |\n| **Duration** | 28 days, 0 hours, 50 minutes |\n| **Orbits** | ~404 |\n\n**Crew:**\n| Name | Role |\n|---|---|\n| Charles \"Pete\" Conrad Jr. | Commander (CDR) |\n| Joseph P. Kerwin | Science Pilot (SPT) — first physician in space |\n| Paul J. Weitz | Pilot (PLT) |\n\n**Key Activities:**\n- Deployed parasol sunshield through scientific airlock to reduce station temperature from 54C to comfortable levels\n- EVA (June 7): Conrad and Kerwin freed jammed solar array wing — 3 hours 25 minutes, longest Earth-orbital spacewalk at that time\n- Total EVAs: 2\n- Set new US spaceflight duration record: 28 days\n\n**Notable Firsts/Records:**\n- First US space station crew\n- First in-orbit major repair of a spacecraft\n- Longest US crewed spaceflight at the time (28 days)\n- \"We can fix anything!\" — crew motto\n\n---\n\n## 1.87 — Skylab 3 (SL-3) — 59 Days\n\n**Mission Type:** Crewed space station mission (extended science)\n**NSSDC ID:** 1973-050A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | July 28, 1973, 11:11 UTC (7:11 AM EDT) |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Saturn IB (SA-207) |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39B |\n| **Splashdown** | September 25, 1973, Pacific Ocean |\n| **Duration** | 59 days, 11 hours, 9 minutes |\n| **Orbits** | 858 |\n\n**Crew:**\n| Name | Role |\n|---|---|\n| Alan L. Bean | Commander (CDR) |\n| Owen K. Garriott | Science Pilot (SPT) |\n| Jack R. Lousma | Pilot (PLT) |\n\n**Key Activities:**\n- Deployed twin-pole sunshade over existing parasol (EVA 1: 6 hrs 31 min — new Earth-orbital EVA record)\n- Replaced ATM film canisters (EVAs 2 and 3)\n- Installed new gyroscopes\n- Three EVAs total; 13 hrs 44 min total EVA time\n- Accomplished 150% of mission science goals\n- Overcame leaky RCS thrusters and crew space motion sickness early in mission\n\n**Science Highlights:**\n- Biomedical experiments on cardiovascular, neurovestibular, musculoskeletal responses\n- EREP Earth observation passes\n- ATM solar observations\n- Crew returned in better physical condition than Skylab 2 crew despite longer mission (improved exercise/nutrition protocols)\n\n**Notable Records:**\n- More than doubled previous US spaceflight duration record (28 to 59 days)\n- Longest Earth-orbital EVA at the time (6 hrs 31 min)\n\n---\n\n## 1.88 — Skylab 4 (SL-4) — 84 Days\n\n**Mission Type:** Crewed space station mission (final/extended)\n**NSSDC ID:** 1973-090A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | November 16, 1973, 14:01 UTC (9:01 AM EST) |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Saturn IB (SA-208) |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center, LC-39B |\n| **Splashdown** | February 8, 1974, Pacific Ocean |\n| **Duration** | 84 days, 1 hour, 16 minutes |\n| **Originally Planned** | 56 days (extended to 84) |\n\n**Crew:**\n| Name | Role | Note |\n|---|---|---|\n| Gerald P. Carr | Commander (CDR) | First spaceflight |\n| Edward G. Gibson | Science Pilot (SPT) | First spaceflight |\n| William R. Pogue | Pilot (PLT) | First spaceflight |\n\nAll three crew members were spaceflight rookies.\n\n**Key Activities:**\n- Four EVAs (record for single Earth-orbital mission at that time): total ~22 hours\n- Comet Kohoutek observations with far-ultraviolet camera (added to instrument suite mid-mission)\n- EVA 3 (3 hrs 29 min) dedicated entirely to photographing Comet Kohoutek at perihelion (Dec 28)\n- Completed significantly more science experiments than planned\n- Boosted Skylab orbit before departure\n\n**Science Highlights:**\n- Biomedical investigations on 84-day spaceflight effects\n- ATM solar observations\n- EREP Earth resources surveys\n- Comet Kohoutek UV, visible, and IR observations\n- Most productive Skylab crew in terms of science return\n\n**Notable Records:**\n- Longest crewed spaceflight at that time (84 days) — record stood until Salyut 6 (1978)\n- Most EVAs in a single mission (4) at that time\n- Final crewed Saturn rocket launch\n- Last US crewed space mission until STS-1 (April 1981)\n\n---\n\n## 1.89 — Mariner 10\n\n**Mission Type:** Venus/Mercury flyby (multi-planet reconnaissance)\n**NSSDC ID:** 1973-085A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | November 3, 1973, 05:45 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas-Centaur (AC-34) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral, LC-36B |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 502.9 kg (1,100 lbs) |\n| **Power System** | Solar panels |\n| **End Date** | March 24, 1975, 12:21 UTC (attitude control gas exhausted) |\n| **Total Duration** | ~17 months |\n| **Cost** | $98 million (first NASA mission with inflexible budget cap) |\n\n**Instruments (7):**\n1. Television Photography System (two telescopes/cameras)\n2. Infrared Radiometer\n3. Ultraviolet Airglow Spectrometer\n4. Ultraviolet Occultation Spectrometer\n5. Magnetometers (two)\n6. Charged Particle Telescope\n7. Plasma Analyzer\n\n**Encounters:**\n\n| Encounter | Date | Closest Approach |\n|---|---|---|\n| Venus flyby | Feb 5, 1974, 17:01 UT | 5,768 km (3,584 mi) |\n| Mercury flyby 1 | Mar 29, 1974, 20:47 UT | 703 km (437 mi) |\n| Mercury flyby 2 | Sep 21, 1974, 20:59 UT | 48,069 km (29,869 mi) |\n| Mercury flyby 3 | Mar 16, 1975, 22:39 UT | 327 km (200 mi) |\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- Venus: 4,165 images returned; first close-up images of cloud-shrouded planet; day-night terminator imaged\n- Mercury: 2,700+ pictures covering nearly half of surface; detected weak magnetic field; Caloris Basin (~1,550 mi diameter); temperature range -183C to 187C; confirmed no atmosphere\n- Observed Comet Kohoutek (January 1974)\n\n**Notable Firsts (mission of many firsts):**\n- First spacecraft to visit Mercury\n- First mission to explore two planets in a single mission\n- First gravity-assist maneuver ever used\n- First return to a primary target for multiple encounters\n- First to use solar wind pressure on solar panels for attitude control\n- First spacecraft data on a long-period comet\n\n**Technology Innovations:**\n- Pioneered gravity-assist technique (saving fuel and enabling smaller launch vehicle)\n- Solar sailing for attitude control (used solar radiation pressure on panels)\n\n---\n\n## 1.90 — Apollo-Soyuz Test Project (ASTP)\n\n**Mission Type:** International crewed docking mission\n**NSSDC ID:** 1975-066A (Apollo), 1975-065A (Soyuz)\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Soyuz Launch** | July 15, 1975, 12:20 UTC from Baikonur Cosmodrome |\n| **Apollo Launch** | July 15, 1975, 19:50 UTC from KSC LC-39B |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Saturn IB (SA-210) for Apollo; Soyuz rocket for Soyuz 19 |\n| **Docking** | July 17, 1975 |\n| **Undocking** | July 19, 1975 |\n| **Apollo Splashdown** | July 24, 1975, 21:18 UTC, Pacific Ocean west of Hawaii |\n| **Soyuz Landing** | July 21, 1975 |\n| **Apollo Mass** | 14,856 kg |\n| **Soyuz Mass** | 6,800 kg |\n| **Apollo Orbit** | 173.3 km apogee, 154.7 km perigee, 87.6-min period, 51.8 deg inclination |\n| **Docked Duration** | 1 day, 23 hours, 7 minutes, 3 seconds (~47 hours) |\n| **Total Apollo Duration** | 9 days, 1 hour, 28 minutes |\n| **Cost** | $245 million US (Soviet cost unknown) |\n\n**Crew:**\n\n| Name | Role | Spacecraft |\n|---|---|---|\n| Thomas P. Stafford | Commander | Apollo |\n| Vance D. Brand | Command Module Pilot | Apollo |\n| Donald K. \"Deke\" Slayton | Docking Module Pilot | Apollo |\n| Aleksey A. Leonov | Commander | Soyuz 19 |\n| Valeriy N. Kubasov | Flight Engineer | Soyuz 19 |\n\n**Docking Module:** Designed/built by MSFC Huntsville; served as airlock and transfer corridor between the two spacecraft (different cabin atmospheres: Apollo pure O2 at 5 psi, Soyuz N2/O2 at ~14.7 psi)\n\n**Experiments (28 total, 5 joint):**\n1. **Artificial Solar Eclipse (MA-148)** — Apollo occulted Sun for Soyuz cameras to photograph solar corona\n2. **Ultraviolet Absorption (UVA)** — measured atomic oxygen/nitrogen at orbital altitudes using retroreflectors\n3. **Zone-Forming Fungi** — microbiology experiment\n4. **Multipurpose Furnace** — materials processing in microgravity\n5. **Earth observation experiments** — continued by Apollo crew after undocking\n- Four crew transfers between spacecraft\n- Exchange of commemorative items\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First international crewed spaceflight\n- First docking of spacecraft from two nations\n- Final Apollo spacecraft flight\n- Final Saturn rocket launch\n- Deke Slayton's first spaceflight (selected as Mercury 7 astronaut in 1959, grounded for heart condition)\n\n---\n\n## 1.91 — Viking 1\n\n**Mission Type:** Mars orbiter + lander (first successful Mars landing)\n**NSSDC IDs:** 1975-075A (orbiter), 1975-075C (lander)\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | August 20, 1975 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Titan IIIE-Centaur |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral |\n| **Total Mass (combined)** | 3,530 kg (fully fueled orbiter+lander) |\n| **Orbiter Mass** | ~900 kg (after separation) |\n| **Lander Mass** | ~600 kg (on surface) |\n| **Orbit Insertion** | June 19, 1976 |\n| **Landing Date** | July 20, 1976, 11:53:06 UT |\n| **Landing Site** | Chryse Planitia (22.27 deg N, 312.05 deg E) |\n| **Orbiter End** | August 7, 1980 (1,489 orbits) |\n| **Lander End** | November 11, 1982 (6+ years on surface) |\n| **Cost** | ~$1 billion (Viking program total, both missions) |\n\n**Orbiter Instruments:**\n1. Visual Imaging System (VIS) — two high-resolution TV cameras (1056x1182 vidicon)\n2. Infrared Thermal Mapper (IRTM) — multichannel radiometer, surface/atmosphere temperatures\n3. Mars Atmospheric Water Detector (MAWD) — IR grating spectrometer for water vapor mapping\n\n**Lander Instruments:**\n1. Two facsimile cameras (imaging system)\n2. Gas Chromatograph Mass Spectrometer (GCMS) — atmospheric composition and organic compound search\n3. X-ray Fluorescence Spectrometer (XRFS) — inorganic soil elemental analysis\n4. Three-axis seismometer\n5. Meteorology package (temperature, pressure, wind speed/direction)\n6. Biology laboratory (three experiments):\n\n**Biology Experiments (detailed):**\n\n| Experiment | Method | Result |\n|---|---|---|\n| **Labeled Release (LR)** | Nutrients tagged with 14C added to soil; monitored for 14CO2 release indicating metabolic activity | Positive response initially; sterilized control showed no response. Debated for decades. |\n| **Gas Exchange (GEX)** | Soil incubated in He/Kr/CO2 atmosphere; nutrient added; monitored for O2, CO2, N2, CH4, H2 changes via gas chromatograph | Rapid O2 release when moistened (attributed to chemical oxidants, not biology) |\n| **Pyrolytic Release (PR)** | Soil exposed to 14C-labeled CO2/CO with simulated sunlight; pyrolyzed at 650C; measured radioactive organic products | Low-level positive in some samples; inconclusive |\n| **GCMS** | Heated soil samples; analyzed evolved gases | No organic compounds detected at ppb level |\n\n**Soil Analysis Results (XRFS):**\n- High iron content; moderate magnesium, calcium, sulfur\n- Low aluminum; very low alkalies and trace elements\n- Mineralogical model: ~80% iron-rich clay, ~10% MgSO4 (kieserite), ~5% carbonate (calcite), ~5% iron oxides\n- Both Viking sites remarkably similar composition (implies homogeneous global regolith)\n\n**Key Findings:**\n- All elements essential to life found (C, N, H, O, P) but no living organisms confirmed\n- Thin CO2 atmosphere, volcanic soil\n- Striking evidence for ancient river beds and vast flooding\n- Later interpretation: perchlorate in soil (discovered by Phoenix) explains both oxidant reactivity and lack of detected organics\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First successful Mars landing\n- First images from Mars surface\n- First in-situ soil analysis on another planet\n\n---\n\n## 1.92 — Viking 2\n\n**Mission Type:** Mars orbiter + lander\n**NSSDC IDs:** 1975-083A (orbiter), 1975-083C (lander)\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | September 9, 1975 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Titan IIIE-Centaur |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral |\n| **Total Mass** | 3,530 kg (same design as Viking 1) |\n| **Lander Mass** | ~600 kg (on surface) |\n| **Orbit Insertion** | August 7, 1976 |\n| **Landing Date** | September 3, 1976 |\n| **Landing Site** | Utopia Planitia (47.97 deg N, 225.71 deg W) — near polar ice cap edge |\n| **Orbiter End** | July 25, 1978 |\n| **Lander End** | April 11, 1980 |\n| **Lander Power** | 620 W nominal |\n\n**Instruments:** Identical to Viking 1 (same orbiter and lander instrument suites)\n\n**Biology Results:**\n- Three biology experiments run on soil collected starting September 12, 1976\n- Results similar to Viking 1: inconclusive — enigmatic chemical activity but no clear evidence of life\n- Scientists attributed reactivity to UV-created soil oxidants (later confirmed: perchlorates)\n\n**Key Differences from Viking 1:**\n- Higher latitude landing site (47.97N vs 22.27N) — closer to polar region\n- Seismometer on Viking 2 operated successfully (Viking 1's failed to uncage)\n- Shorter operational life than Viking 1 (lander: 3.5 years vs 6+ years)\n\n---\n\n## 1.93 — Voyager 2\n\n**Mission Type:** Outer planet Grand Tour\n**NSSDC ID:** 1977-076A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | August 20, 1977 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Titan IIIE-Centaur |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 721.9 kg |\n| **Dimensions** | Decahedral bus: 47 cm height, 1.78 m across; 3.66 m diameter high-gain antenna |\n| **Power** | Three MHW-RTGs, ~470 W total at launch (Pu-238); each RTG: 40.6 cm diameter, 50.8 cm length, 39 kg |\n| **Cost** | $865 million (Voyager 1+2 combined through Neptune) + $30M Interstellar Mission |\n| **Status** | Still operating in interstellar space |\n\n**Instruments (10 investigations + radio science = 11):**\n\n| Instrument | Acronym | Status |\n|---|---|---|\n| Imaging Science Subsystem | ISS | Inactive |\n| Infrared Interferometer Spectrometer | IRIS | Inactive |\n| Photopolarimeter Subsystem | PPS | Inactive |\n| Ultraviolet Spectrometer | UVS | Inactive |\n| Plasma Science | PLS | Inactive |\n| Planetary Radio Astronomy | PRA | Inactive |\n| Cosmic Ray Subsystem | CRS | Active (as of 2025, being turned off) |\n| Low-Energy Charged Particles | LECP | Active (as of 2025, being turned off) |\n| Magnetometer | MAG | Active |\n| Plasma Wave Subsystem | PWS | Active |\n| Radio Science System | RSS | (uses telecom system) |\n\n**Trajectory & Encounters:**\n\n| Planet | Date | Closest Approach |\n|---|---|---|\n| **Jupiter** | July 9, 1979 | 650,180 km |\n| **Saturn** | August 25, 1981 | 41,000 km |\n| **Uranus** | January 24, 1986 | 81,500 km |\n| **Neptune** | August 25, 1989 | ~4,950 km (above north pole) |\n\n**Key Discoveries at Each Planet:**\n\n**Jupiter (1979):**\n- Contributed to 33,000+ combined Jupiter images\n- Active volcanism on Io confirmed (9 erupting volcanoes; plumes 300+ km high)\n- Europa's icy surface suggesting subsurface ocean\n- Io's tidal heating mechanism (tidal pumping from Europa, Ganymede, and Jupiter)\n\n**Saturn (1981):**\n- Upper atmosphere: ~7% helium, remainder hydrogen\n- Equatorial wind speeds: ~500 m/s (1,100 mph)\n- Fine ring structure revealed\n- Aurora-like UV emissions at mid-latitudes\n- Temperature: 82K at 70 mbar to 143K at 1,200 mbar\n\n**Uranus (1986):**\n- 11 previously unseen moons discovered\n- Two new rings detected\n- Rotation period: 17 hrs 14 min\n- Magnetic field tilted 59 degrees from rotational axis, offset 1/3 radius from center (unique)\n- Miranda: varied landscape with evidence of past geologic activity; closest approach 29,000 km\n- Equatorial and polar temperatures equal despite extreme axial tilt\n\n**Neptune (1989):**\n- Great Dark Spot (storm similar to Jupiter's Great Red Spot)\n- Six new moons discovered\n- Four rings detected\n- Triton: pink methane ice polar cap; active nitrogen geysers (5-mile-tall plumes)\n- Closest planetary flyby of mission (4,950 km)\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- Only spacecraft to visit all four outer planets\n- Only spacecraft to visit Uranus and Neptune\n- Launched before Voyager 1 but numbered \"2\" due to slower trajectory\n\n**Golden Record:**\n- 12-inch gold-plated copper disk\n- 115 images encoded in analog form\n- Greetings in 55 languages (Akkadian to Wu)\n- 35 natural sounds (thunder, birds, whales, etc.)\n- 27 musical selections (~90 minutes, Eastern/Western classics + ethnic music)\n- Printed messages from President Carter and UN Secretary General Waldheim\n- Cover includes instructions for playback, finding Earth, and dating\n- Inscription: \"To the makers of music -- all worlds, all time\"\n- Assembled by committee chaired by Carl Sagan\n- Playback speed: 1000 rpm\n\n---\n\n## 1.94 — Voyager 1\n\n**Mission Type:** Outer planet flyby / interstellar probe\n**NSSDC ID:** 1977-084A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | September 5, 1977 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Titan IIIE-Centaur |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | ~721.9 kg (identical to Voyager 2) |\n| **Power** | Three MHW-RTGs, ~470 W at launch |\n| **Status** | Farthest human-made object; in interstellar space since August 2012 |\n\n**Instruments:** Identical to Voyager 2 (10 instruments + radio science)\n\n**Trajectory & Encounters:**\n\n| Planet | Date | Closest Approach |\n|---|---|---|\n| **Jupiter** | March 5, 1979 | 277,400 km (174,000 mi from cloud tops) |\n| **Saturn** | November 12, 1980 | 64,200 km |\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n\n**Jupiter (1979):**\n- Nearly 19,000 images\n- Discovered thin ring system around Jupiter\n- Discovered two new moons: Thebe and Metis\n- Active volcanism on Io (first active volcanoes beyond Earth)\n- Europa's fractured ice surface\n- Sulfur, oxygen, sodium from Io detected at magnetosphere's outer edge\n\n**Saturn (1980):**\n- Five new moons discovered\n- G-ring discovered\n- Detailed study of Saturn's rings, atmosphere, and magnetosphere\n- Titan encounter: close flyby revealed thick nitrogen atmosphere (precluded Uranus/Neptune trajectory — mission redirected for Titan priority)\n\n**Interstellar Milestones:**\n- Overtook Voyager 2 on December 15, 1977 (faster trajectory)\n- \"Pale Blue Dot\" image taken February 14, 1990 at 6 billion km\n- Crossed heliopause into interstellar space: August 25, 2012\n- Fastest human-made object: ~17 km/s (~61,200 km/h)\n- As of 2025: four instruments still returning data (CRS, LECP, MAG, PWS) — two being shut down March 2025\n\n---\n\n## 1.95 — Pioneer Venus Orbiter (Pioneer Venus 1)\n\n**Mission Type:** Venus orbiter\n**NSSDC ID:** 1978-051A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | May 20, 1978, 13:13 UTC |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas-Centaur (AC-50) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral, LC-36A |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 582 kg (1,283 lbs) |\n| **Design** | Cylindrical bus, 2.5 m diameter, 1.2 m high |\n| **Orbit Insertion** | December 4, 1978, 15:58 UT |\n| **Initial Orbit Period** | 23 hrs 11 min (adjusted to 24 hrs within 2 orbits) |\n| **Periapsis** | ~160 km (99 mi) |\n| **Mission End** | October 8, 1992, 19:22 UT (burned up in atmosphere) |\n| **Total Duration** | 14 years (planned: 8 months) |\n| **Cost** | $125 million (orbiter build + 10 years operations) |\n\n**Instruments (16):**\n1. Charged Particle Retarding Potential Analyzer (ORPA)\n2. Ion Mass Spectrometer (OIMS)\n3. Thermal Electron Temperature Langmuir Probe (OETP)\n4. Neutral Particle Mass Spectrometer (ONMS)\n5. Cloud Photopolarimeter (OCPP)\n6. Temperature Sounding Infrared Radiometer (OIR)\n7. Magnetic Field Fluxgate Magnetometer (OMAG)\n8. Solar Wind Plasma Analyzer (OPA)\n9. Surface Radar Mapper (ORAD)\n10. Electric Field Detector (OEFD)\n11. Transient Gamma Ray Burst Detector (OGBD)\n12. Radio Occultation Experiment\n13. Atmospheric and Solar Corona Turbulence Experiment\n14. Drag Measurements Experiment\n15. Two Radio Science Experiments (gravity field)\n16. Ultraviolet Spectrometer (OUVS)\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- Radar mapped 93% of surface (73N to 63S) at ~75 km resolution\n- Discovered Maxwell Montes: 10.8 km above mean surface (highest point on Venus, taller than Everest)\n- Clouds consist mainly of sulfuric acid\n- Almost continuous lightning in atmosphere\n- Venus has minimal/no intrinsic magnetic field\n- Dark cloud markings in UV; infrared clearing over north pole\n- First American spacecraft to orbit Venus\n\n---\n\n## 1.96 — Pioneer Venus Multiprobe (Pioneer Venus 2)\n\n**Mission Type:** Venus atmospheric probe mission\n**NSSDC ID:** 1978-078A (bus), 1978-078D (large probe), 1978-078E/F/G (small probes)\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | August 8, 1978, 07:33 UT |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas-Centaur (AC-51) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral, LC-36A |\n| **Bus Mass** | 904 kg (1,993 lbs) |\n| **Large Probe Mass** | 316.5 kg (698 lbs), 1.5 m diameter |\n| **Small Probe Mass** | ~93 kg each, 0.76 m diameter |\n| **Cost** | $83 million (probes build + operations) |\n| **Total Pioneer Venus Cost** | ~$200 million (orbiter + multiprobe combined) |\n\n**Probe Release & Entry:**\n- Large probe released: November 16, 1978 (7 million miles from Venus)\n- Small probes released: November 20, 1978 (6 million miles from Venus)\n- All probes entered atmosphere: December 9, 1978\n- Bus burned up at ~120 km altitude, 1.5 hours after probe entry\n\n**Instruments:**\n\n*Bus:*\n1. Neutral Mass Spectrometer (BNMS)\n2. Ion Mass Spectrometer (BIMS)\n\n*Large Probe:*\n1. Neutral Mass Spectrometer\n2. Solar Flux Radiometer\n3. Gas Chromatograph\n4. Infrared Radiometer\n5. Cloud Particle Size Spectrometer\n6. Nephelometer\n7. Atmospheric structure experiment (T, P, acceleration)\n\n*Each Small Probe:*\n1. Nephelometer\n2. Net Flux Radiometer\n3. Temperature, Pressure, Acceleration sensors\n\n**Landing Sites & Survival:**\n\n| Probe | Coordinates | Survival on Surface |\n|---|---|---|\n| Large Probe | 4.4N, 304E | Ceased at impact (as designed, no surface capability) |\n| North Probe | 60N, 4E | Survived impact briefly |\n| Day Probe | 32S, 318E | **67 minutes 37 seconds** on surface |\n| Night Probe | 27S, 56E | Survived impact briefly |\n\nEntry velocity: 11.6 km/s; descent time: 53-56 minutes each; instrument activation: ~70 km altitude\n\n**Key Atmospheric Discoveries:**\n- Below 50 km: minimal temperature variation across all probe sites (thousands of miles apart)\n- Below 30 km: atmosphere relatively clear beneath haze layer\n- 10-50 km: almost no convection\n- Confirmed extreme surface conditions (460C, 90 atm)\n\n---\n\n## 1.97 — ISEE-3 / ICE (International Cometary Explorer)\n\n**Mission Type:** Sun-Earth interaction study / first comet flyby\n**NSSDC ID:** 1978-079A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | August 12, 1978, 15:12 UT |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Delta 2914 (Thor no. 633) |\n| **Launch Site** | Cape Canaveral, LC-17B |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 479 kg (1,056 lbs) |\n| **Power** | 173 W nominal |\n| **Halo Orbit Achieved** | November 20, 1978, at L1 Sun-Earth Lagrange point (~1.5 million km from Earth) |\n| **Comet Flyby** | September 11, 1985, 11:02 UT — Comet 21P/Giacobini-Zinner, 7,862 km from nucleus |\n| **Mission End** | May 5, 1997 (NASA terminated operations) |\n| **Total Duration** | ~19 years |\n\n**Instruments (14):**\n1. Solar Wind Plasma Detector\n2. Vector Helium Magnetometer\n3. Low Energy Cosmic Ray Experiment\n4. Medium Energy Cosmic Ray Experiment\n5. High Energy Cosmic Ray Experiment\n6. Plasma Waves Spectrum Analyzer\n7. Energetic Particle Anisotropy Spectrometer\n8. Interplanetary and Solar Electrons Experiment\n9. Radio Mapping of Solar Disturbances Experiment\n10. Solar Wind Ion Composition Experiment\n11. Cosmic Ray Isotope Spectrometer\n12. X-rays and Gamma-ray Bursts Experiment\n13. Gamma-Ray Bursts Experiment\n14. Cosmic-ray Energy Spectrum Charged-Particle Telescope\n\n**Mission Phases:**\n1. **1978-1982:** Halo orbit at L1 — studied Sun-Earth interactions, solar wind\n2. **1982-1983:** Redirected through Earth's magnetospheric tail (pioneered trajectory using 5 lunar gravity assists; last Moon flyby Dec 22, 1983 at ~120 km range)\n3. **1985:** Comet Giacobini-Zinner encounter\n4. **1986:** Solar wind data near Halley's Comet (~25 million miles)\n5. **2014:** Brief reboot attempt by citizen scientists\n\n**Key Discoveries:**\n- Confirmed comets are \"dirty snowballs\" (ice + dust composition)\n- Detected huge plasmoid ejected from Earth's magnetosphere\n- First spacecraft in halo orbit around a Lagrange point\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First spacecraft to orbit a Lagrange point (L1)\n- First spacecraft to fly past a comet\n- Pioneered complex multi-lunar-gravity-assist trajectory (Robert Farquhar)\n- Renamed ICE in 1983 for cometary phase\n\n---\n\n## 1.98 — HEAO-1 (High Energy Astronomy Observatory 1)\n\n**Mission Type:** X-ray sky survey\n**NSSDC ID:** 1977-075A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | August 12, 1977 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas-Centaur |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 2,552 kg (includes 1,220 kg experiments) |\n| **Dimensions** | 5.68 m high, 2.67 m diameter (hexagonal) |\n| **Orbit** | ~445 km apogee, 22.75 deg inclination, ~93-min period, near-circular |\n| **Mission End** | January 9, 1979 (attitude control gas exhausted); reentry March 1979 |\n| **Duration** | ~17 months |\n| **Cost** | ~$83M (HEAO program total ~$250M for all three) |\n\n**Instruments (4):**\n1. **A1 — Large Area X-ray Survey (NRL)** — scanning survey instrument\n2. **A2 — Cosmic X-ray Background Experiment** — 6 proportional counters + 1 scintillation counter (0.2-60 keV)\n3. **A3 — Scanning Modulation Collimator (MC LASS)** — precise source positioning\n4. **A4 — Hard X-ray and Low Energy Gamma-Ray Experiment** — NaI/CsI phoswich detectors (15 keV - 10 MeV)\n\n**Energy Range:** 0.2 keV to 10 MeV\n\n**Operation:** Rotated once per 30 minutes about Earth-Sun line (scanning mission); surveyed X-ray sky nearly three times\n\n**Key Results:**\n- A1 catalog: 842 X-ray sources detected (limiting flux 250 nJy at 5 keV) from first 6 months\n- Nearly continuous monitoring of ecliptic pole sources\n- Source categories: narrow/broad emission line galaxies, BL Lac objects, galaxy clusters\n- Provided first comprehensive all-sky X-ray survey above previous missions' sensitivity\n\n---\n\n## 1.99 — HEAO-2 / Einstein Observatory\n\n**Mission Type:** First imaging X-ray telescope\n**NSSDC ID:** 1978-103A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | November 13, 1978 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas-Centaur |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | 3,130 kg |\n| **Orbit** | Low Earth orbit |\n| **Mission End** | April 1981 |\n| **Duration** | ~2.5 years |\n| **Angular Resolution** | Few arcseconds (HRI: 2 arcsec within 5 arcmin of axis) |\n| **Sensitivity** | Several hundred times greater than any previous X-ray experiment |\n\n**Instruments (5 at focal plane, interchangeable via carousel):**\n\n| Instrument | Acronym | FOV | Energy Range | Resolution |\n|---|---|---|---|---|\n| High Resolution Imaging Detector | HRI | 25' diameter | 0.15-3.0 keV | 2 arcsec, 8 ms time |\n| Imaging Proportional Counter | IPC | 75'x75' | 0.4-4.0 keV | ~1 arcmin, 63 ms time |\n| Solid State Spectrometer | SSS | — | 0.5-4.5 keV | 160 eV FWHM |\n| Focal Plane Crystal Spectrometer | FPCS | — | 0.42-2.6 keV | 50-1000 eV |\n| Monitor Proportional Counter | MPC | 1.5 deg | 1-20 keV | 667 cm2 area |\n\n**Telescope:** Large grazing-incidence X-ray optics (Wolter Type I nested mirrors)\n\n**Key Results:**\n- 5,000+ targeted observations\n- Several thousand additional serendipitous sources discovered\n- Observed: AGNs, X-ray binaries, cataclysmic variables, supernova remnants, normal stars, galaxy clusters\n- First X-ray observatory with Guest Observer Program\n- Revolutionized X-ray astronomy from a specialist field to mainstream astrophysics\n\n**Notable Firsts:**\n- First fully imaging focusing X-ray telescope in space\n- First to achieve arcsecond X-ray imaging resolution\n- Named \"Einstein\" after launch (born 1879, centenary year)\n\n---\n\n## 1.100 — HEAO-3\n\n**Mission Type:** Gamma-ray spectroscopy and cosmic ray survey\n**NSSDC ID:** 1979-082A\n\n| Field | Detail |\n|---|---|\n| **Launch Date** | September 20, 1979 |\n| **Launch Vehicle** | Atlas-Centaur |\n| **Launch Site** | Kennedy Space Center |\n| **Orbit** | 500 km altitude, 43.6 deg inclination |\n| **Mission End** | May 29, 1981 |\n| **Duration** | ~20 months |\n| **Energy Range** | 50 keV - 10 MeV |\n| **Spacecraft Mass** | ~2,660 kg (estimated, similar to HEAO-1 class) |\n\n**Instruments (3):**\n\n1. **High Resolution Gamma Ray Spectrometer (HRGRS)** — JPL/UCSD\n   - Four p-type high-purity germanium detectors, each ~100 cm3\n   - Energy range: 50 keV - 10 MeV\n   - Field of view: 30 degrees\n   - Effective area: 75 cm2 at 100 keV\n   - Largest germanium spectrometer placed in orbit at that time\n\n2. **Cosmic Ray Isotope Experiment** — Danish-French collaboration (DSRI/Saclay)\n   - Measured isotopic composition of cosmic rays\n\n3. **Heavy Nuclei Experiment** — Caltech, Washington University, University of Minnesota\n   - Among the largest cosmic-ray detectors flown in space\n   - Measured ultra-heavy cosmic ray nuclei\n\n**Key Results:**\n- Sky survey of gamma-ray narrow-line emission\n- Detected gamma-ray bursts (notably double burst event November 16, 1979)\n- Cosmic ray isotopic composition measurements\n- Heavy nuclei abundance measurements (iron-group through actinides)\n\n**Notable:**\n- Final HEAO mission\n- Completed the HEAO program's comprehensive survey from soft X-rays through gamma rays to cosmic rays\n\n---\n\n## Summary Table\n\n| Ver | Mission | Launch | End | Duration | Mass (kg) | Cost ($M) | Key First |\n|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|\n| 1.84 | Pioneer 11 |
